   <div class="mw-indicators">

   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Graphene
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote">
      Not to be confused with
      <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">
       Graphite
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Grapheme" title="Grapheme">
       Grapheme
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Graphane" title="Graphane">
       Graphane
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Graphyne" title="Graphyne">
       Graphyne
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graphen.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1280" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Graphen.jpg/220px-Graphen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Graphen.jpg/330px-Graphen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Graphen.jpg/440px-Graphen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graphen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Graphene is an
        <a href="/wiki/Chicken_wire_(chemistry)" title="Chicken wire (chemistry)">
         atomic-scale honeycomb lattice
        </a>
        made of
        <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
         carbon
        </a>
        atoms.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Graphene
      </b>
      (/rf.in/)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      is an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotrope" title="Allotrope">
       allotrope
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
       carbon
      </a>
      in the form of a
      <a href="/wiki/2D_Materials" title="2D Materials">
       two-dimensional
      </a>
      , atomic-scale, hexagonal lattice in which one atom forms each
      <a href="/wiki/Vertex_(geometry)" title="Vertex (geometry)">
       vertex
      </a>
      . It is the basic structural element of other allotropes, including
      <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">
       graphite
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">
       charcoal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">
       carbon nanotubes
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">
       fullerenes
      </a>
      . It can also be considered as an indefinitely large
      <a href="/wiki/Aromaticity" title="Aromaticity">
       aromatic
      </a>
      molecule, the limiting case
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2015)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      of the family of flat
      <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">
       polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;">
       <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:330px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" style="width:854px;height:480px" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Graphene_and_Dirac_Cones.ogv/854px--Graphene_and_Dirac_Cones.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" class="kskin" data-durationhint="135.14666666667" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Graphene_and_Dirac_Cones.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f4/Graphene_and_Dirac_Cones.ogv/Graphene_and_Dirac_Cones.ogv.720p.webm" type="video/webm;           </span>

        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graphene_and_Dirac_Cones.ogv" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Graphene and its
        <a href="/wiki/Electronic_band_structure" title="Electronic band structure">
         band structure
        </a>
        and Dirac Cones, effect of a grid on
        <a href="/wiki/Doping_(semiconductor)" title="Doping (semiconductor)">
         doping
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Graphene has many extraordinary properties. It is about 200 times stronger than steel by weight,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yahoo.155243022_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-yahoo.155243022-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      conducts heat and electricity with great efficiency and is nearly transparent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Researchers have identified the
      <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_junction_transistor" title="Bipolar junction transistor">
       bipolar transistor
      </a>
      effect,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ballistic_transport" title="Ballistic transport">
       ballistic transport
      </a>
      of charges and large quantum oscillations in the material.
     </p>
     <p>
      Scientists have theorized about graphene for decades. It is quite likely that graphene was unwittingly produced in small quantities for centuries through the use of pencils and other similar applications of graphite, but it was first measurably produced and isolated in the lab in 2003.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APS_News_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-APS_News-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Research was informed by existing theoretical descriptions of its composition, structure and properties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      High-quality graphene proved to be surprisingly easy to isolate, making more research possible.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Andre_Geim" title="Andre Geim">
       Andre Geim
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Novoselov" title="Konstantin Novoselov">
       Konstantin Novoselov
      </a>
      at the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manchester" title="University of Manchester">
       University of Manchester
      </a>
      won the
      <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">
       Nobel Prize in Physics
      </a>
      in 2010 "for groundbreaking experiments regarding the
      <a href="/wiki/Two-dimensional_space" title="Two-dimensional space">
       two-dimensional
      </a>
      material graphene."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The global market for graphene is reported to have reached $9 million by 2014 with most sales in the semiconductor, electronics, battery energy and composites industries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">
       Definition
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      "Graphene" is a combination of graphite and the suffix
      <a href="/wiki/-ene" title="-ene">
       -ene
      </a>
      , named by
      <a href="/wiki/Hanns-Peter_Boehm" title="Hanns-Peter Boehm">
       Hanns-Peter Boehm
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-termorigin_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-termorigin-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      who described single-layer carbon foils in 1962.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boehm1962_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Boehm1962-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The term
      <i>
       graphene
      </i>
      first appeared in 1987
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mouras87_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mouras87-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to describe single sheets of graphite as a constituent of
      <a href="/wiki/Graphite_intercalation_compound" title="Graphite intercalation compound">
       graphite intercalation compounds
      </a>
      (GICs); conceptually a GIC is a crystalline salt of the intercalant and graphene. The term was also used in early descriptions of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">
       carbon nanotubes
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saito92_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Saito92-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epitaxial" title="Epitaxial">
       epitaxial
      </a>
      graphene
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbeaux98_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Forbeaux98-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang00_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wang00-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene can be considered an "infinite alternant" (only six-member
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_ring" title="Carbon ring">
       carbon ring
      </a>
      )
      <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">
       polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon
      </a>
      (PAH).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">
       IUPAC
      </a>
      compendium of technology states: "previously, descriptions such as graphite layers, carbon layers, or carbon sheets have been used for the term graphene... it is incorrect to use for a single layer a term which includes the term graphite, which would imply a three-dimensional structure. The term graphene should be used only when the reactions, structural relations or other properties of individual layers are discussed."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iupac-gold-book_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-iupac-gold-book-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Geim defined "isolated or free-standing graphene" as "graphene is a single atomic plane of graphite, which and this is essential is sufficiently isolated from its environment to be considered free-standing."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sciencerev09_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sciencerev09-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This definition is narrower than the IUPAC definition and refers to cloven, transferred, and suspended graphene.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Other forms of graphene, such as graphene grown on various metals, can become free-standing if, for example, suspended or transferred to
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">
       silicon dioxide
      </a>
      (
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      ) or
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">
       silicon carbide
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SiCplusH2_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SiCplusH2-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In 1859
      <a href="/wiki/Sir_Benjamin_Collins_Brodie,_2nd_Baronet" title="Sir Benjamin Collins Brodie, 2nd Baronet">
       Benjamin Collins Brodie
      </a>
      was aware of the highly
      <a href="/wiki/Lamella_(materials)" title="Lamella (materials)">
       lamellar
      </a>
      structure of thermally reduced
      <a href="/wiki/Graphite_oxide" title="Graphite oxide">
       graphite oxide
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The structure of graphite was solved in 1916
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      by the related method of
      <a href="/wiki/Powder_diffraction" title="Powder diffraction">
       powder diffraction
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was studied in detail by V. Kohlschtter and P. Haenni in 1918, who also described the properties of
      <a href="/wiki/Graphene_oxide_paper" title="Graphene oxide paper">
       graphite oxide paper
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kohlschuttler1918_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kohlschuttler1918-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Its structure was determined from single-crystal diffraction in 1924.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The theory of graphene was first explored by
      <a href="/wiki/P._R._Wallace" title="P. R. Wallace">
       P. R. Wallace
      </a>
      in 1947 as a starting point for understanding the electronic properties of 3D graphite. The emergent massless Dirac equation was first pointed out by
      <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Walter_Semenoff" title="Gordon Walter Semenoff">
       Gordon Walter Semenoff
      </a>
      and David P. DeVincenzo and Eugene J. Mele.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-devincenzo_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-devincenzo-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Semenoff emphasized the occurrence in a magnetic field of an electronic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landau_level" title="Landau level">
       Landau level
      </a>
      precisely at the Dirac point. This level is responsible for the anomalous integer quantum Hall effect.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2dgasDiracFermions_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-2dgasDiracFermions-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gusynin_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gusynin-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berry.27sPhase_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Berry.27sPhase-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The earliest TEM images of few-layer graphite were published by G. Ruess and F. Vogt in 1948.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuessTEM_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RuessTEM-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later, single graphene layers were also observed directly by electron microscopy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer07_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Meyer07-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before 2004 intercalated graphite compounds were studied under a
      <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">
       transmission electron microscope
      </a>
      (TEM). Researchers occasionally observed thin graphitic flakes ("few-layer graphene") and possibly even individual layers. An early, detailed study on few-layer graphite dates to 1962.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GroxTEM_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GroxTEM-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Starting in the 1970s single layers of graphite were grown epitaxially on top of other materials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oshima97_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Oshima97-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This "epitaxial graphene" consists of a single-atom-thick hexagonal lattice of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sp2_bond" title="Sp2 bond">
       sp
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       -bonded
      </a>
      carbon atoms, as in free-standing graphene. However, there is significant charge transfer from the substrate to the epitaxial graphene, and, in some cases, hybridization between the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-orbital" title="D-orbital">
       d-orbitals
      </a>
      of the substrate atoms and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pi_orbital" title="Pi orbital">
        orbitals
      </a>
      of graphene, which significantly alters the electronic structure of epitaxial graphene.
     </p>
     <p>
      Single layers of graphite were also observed by
      <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">
       transmission electron microscopy
      </a>
      within bulk materials, in particular inside soot obtained by chemical exfoliation. Efforts to make thin films of graphite by mechanical exfoliation started in 1990,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciAm_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SciAm-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but nothing thinner than 50 to 100 layers was produced before 2004.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.tif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nobelpriset_i_fysik_2010.tif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A lump of
        <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">
         graphite
        </a>
        , a graphene
        <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">
         transistor
        </a>
        and a
        <a href="/wiki/Tape_dispenser" title="Tape dispenser">
         tape dispenser
        </a>
        . Donated to the
        <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Museum" title="Nobel Museum">
         Nobel Museum
        </a>
        in Stockholm by
        <a href="/wiki/Andre_Geim" title="Andre Geim">
         Andre Geim
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Novoselov" title="Konstantin Novoselov">
         Konstantin Novoselov
        </a>
        in 2010.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Initial attempts to make atomically thin graphitic films employed exfoliation techniques similar to the drawing method. Multilayer samples down to 10nm in thickness were obtained.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Old papers were unearthed
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GroxTEM_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GroxTEM-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in which researchers tried to isolate graphene starting with intercalated compounds. These papers reported the observation of very thin graphitic fragments (possibly monolayers) by transmission electron microscopy. Neither of the earlier observations was sufficient to "spark the graphene gold rush," which awaited macroscopic samples of extracted atomic planes.
     </p>
     <p>
      One of the very first patents pertaining to the production of graphene was filed in October 2002 and granted in 2006 (US Pat. 7071258).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patent_7071258_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Patent_7071258-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Titled, "Nano-scaled Graphene Plates," this patent detailed one of the very first large scale graphene production processes. Two years later, in 2004
      <a href="/wiki/Andre_Geim" title="Andre Geim">
       Andre Geim
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kostya_Novoselov" title="Kostya Novoselov">
       Kostya Novoselov
      </a>
      at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_University_of_Manchester" title="The University of Manchester">
       The University of Manchester
      </a>
      extracted single-atom-thick crystallites from bulk graphite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nov_04_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nov_04-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They pulled graphene layers from graphite and transferred them onto thin
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      on a silicon wafer in a process called either micromechanical cleavage or the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scotch_tape" title="Scotch tape">
       Scotch tape
      </a>
      technique.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Story_of_Graphene_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-The_Story_of_Graphene-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      electrically isolated the graphene and weakly interacted with it, providing nearly charge-neutral graphene layers. The silicon beneath the
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      could be used as a "back gate" electrode to vary the charge density in the graphene over a wide range. They may not have been the first to use this technique
      <span class="citation patent">
       <a class="external text" href="http://worldwide.espacenet.com/textdoc?DB=EPODOC&amp;IDX=US6667100" rel="nofollow">
        US 6667100
       </a>
      </span>
      <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Apatent&amp;rft.number=6667100&amp;rft.cc=US&amp;rft.title=">
       <span style="display: none;">
       </span>
      </span>
      , filed in 2002, describes how to process commercially available flexible expanded graphite to achieve a graphite thickness of 0.01 thousandth of an inch. The key to success was high-throughput visual recognition of graphene on a properly chosen substrate, which provides a small but noticeable optical contrast.
     </p>
     <p>
      The cleavage technique led directly to the first observation of the anomalous quantum Hall effect in graphene,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2dgasDiracFermions_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-2dgasDiracFermions-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berry.27sPhase_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Berry.27sPhase-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which provided direct evidence of graphene's theoretically predicted
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berry%27s_phase" title="Berry's phase">
       Berry's phase
      </a>
      of massless
      <a href="/wiki/Dirac_fermion" title="Dirac fermion">
       Dirac fermions
      </a>
      . The effect was reported soon after by
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_Kim" title="Philip Kim">
       Philip Kim
      </a>
      and Yuanbo Zhang in 2005. These experiments started after the researchers observed colleagues who were looking for the quantum Hall effect
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Dirac fermions
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in bulk graphite.
     </p>
     <p>
      Even though graphene on nickel and on silicon carbide have both existed in the laboratory for decades, graphene mechanically exfoliated on
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      provided the first proof of the Dirac fermion nature of electrons.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2180" data-file-width="3270" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg/220px-Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg/330px-Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg/440px-Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nobel_Prize_2010-Press_Conference_KVA-DSC_8009.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Andre_Geim" title="Andre Geim">
         Andre Geim
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Novoselov" title="Konstantin Novoselov">
         Konstantin Novoselov
        </a>
        , 2010
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Geim and Novoselov received several awards for their pioneering research on graphene, notably the 2010
      <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">
       Nobel Prize in Physics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2014 a 60m
      <a href="/wiki/National_Graphene_Institute" title="National Graphene Institute">
       National Graphene Institute
      </a>
      a 60m Graphene Engineering Innovation Centre (GEIC) were announced to support applied research and development in partnership with other research organisations and industry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">
       North East England
      </a>
      two commercial manufacturers, Applied Graphene Materials
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Thomas Swan Limited,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (with Trinity College, Dublin researchers)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      have begun manufacturing. In
      <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">
       East Anglia
      </a>
      , another manufacturer, Cambridge Nanosystems, is operating a large scale graphene powder production facilities.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">
       Properties
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Properties">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Graphene has a theoretical
      <a href="/wiki/Specific_surface_area" title="Specific surface area">
       specific surface area
      </a>
      (SSA) of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7006263000000000000
       </span>
       2630
       <a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">
        m
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </a>
       /g
      </span>
      . This is much larger than that reported to date for carbon black (typically smaller than
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7005900000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        900
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">
        m
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </a>
       /g
      </span>
      ) or for carbon nanotubes (CNTs), from 100 to
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7006100000000000000
       </span>
       1000
       <a href="/wiki/Square_metre" title="Square metre">
        m
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </a>
       /g
      </span>
      and is similar to
      <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">
       activated carbon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">
       Structure
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Structure">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graphene_SPM.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="875" data-file-width="626" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Graphene_SPM.jpg/220px-Graphene_SPM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Graphene_SPM.jpg/330px-Graphene_SPM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Graphene_SPM.jpg/440px-Graphene_SPM.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graphene_SPM.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Scanning_probe_microscopy" title="Scanning probe microscopy">
         Scanning probe microscopy
        </a>
        image of graphene
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Graphene is a
      <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">
       crystalline
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotrope" title="Allotrope">
       allotrope
      </a>
      of carbon with 2-dimensional properties. Its carbon atoms are densely packed in a regular
      <a href="/wiki/Chicken_wire_(chemistry)" title="Chicken wire (chemistry)">
       atomic-scale chicken wire
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_tiling" title="Hexagonal tiling">
       hexagonal
      </a>
      ) pattern.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erog11_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-erog11-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Each atom has four bonds, one  bond with each of its three neighbors and one -bond that is oriented out of plane. The atoms are about 1.42  apart.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erog11_47-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-erog11-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene's hexagonal lattice can be regarded as two interleaving triangular lattices. This perspective was successfully used to calculate the band structure for a single graphite layer using a tight-binding approximation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erog11_47-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-erog11-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene's stability is due to its tightly packed carbon atoms and an sp
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_hybridization" title="Orbital hybridization">
       orbital hybridization
      </a>
       a combination of orbitals s, p
      <sub>
       x
      </sub>
      and p
      <sub>
       y
      </sub>
      that constitute the -bond. The final p
      <sub>
       z
      </sub>
      electron makes up the -bond. The -bonds hybridize together to form the -band and -bands. These bands are responsible for most of graphene's notable electronic properties, via the half-filled band that permits free-moving electrons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erog11_47-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-erog11-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene sheets in solid form usually show evidence in diffraction for graphite's (002) layering. This is true of some single-walled nanostructures.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, unlayered graphene with only (hk0) rings has been found in the core of
      <a href="/wiki/Presolar_grains" title="Presolar grains">
       presolar
      </a>
      graphite onions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      TEM studies show faceting at defects in flat graphene sheets
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and suggest a role for two-dimensional crystallization from a melt.
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene can self-repair holes in its sheets, when exposed to molecules containing carbon, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">
       hydrocarbons
      </a>
      . Bombarded with pure carbon atoms, the atoms perfectly align into
      <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">
       hexagons
      </a>
      , completely filling the holes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manchester_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Manchester-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zme_20120712_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-zme_20120712-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_structure" title="Atomic structure">
       atomic structure
      </a>
      of isolated, single-layer graphene was studied by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscope" title="Transmission electron microscope">
       transmission electron microscopy
      </a>
      (TEM) on sheets of graphene suspended between bars of a metallic grid.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer07_30-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Meyer07-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Electron diffraction patterns showed the expected honeycomb lattice. Suspended graphene also showed "rippling" of the flat sheet, with amplitude of about one nanometer. These ripples may be intrinsic to the material as a result of the instability of two-dimensional crystals,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlsson_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Carlsson-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fasolino_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fasolino-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or may originate from the ubiquitous dirt seen in all TEM images of graphene. Atomic resolution real-space images of isolated, single-layer graphene on
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      substrates are available
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ishigami07_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ishigami07-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stolyarova_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stolyarova-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      via
      <a href="/wiki/Scanning_tunneling_microscope" title="Scanning tunneling microscope">
       scanning tunneling microscopy
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Photoresist" title="Photoresist">
       Photoresist
      </a>
      residue, which must be removed to obtain atomic-resolution images, may be the "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adsorbate" title="Adsorbate">
       adsorbates
      </a>
      " observed in TEM images, and may explain the observed rippling. Rippling on
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      is caused by conformation of graphene to the underlying
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      , and is not intrinsic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ishigami07_55-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ishigami07-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">
       Chemical
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Chemical">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Graphene is the only form of carbon (or solid material) in which every atom is available for chemical reaction from two sides (due to the 2D structure). Atoms at the edges of a graphene sheet have special chemical reactivity. Graphene has the highest ratio of edge atoms of any allotrope. Defects within a sheet increase its chemical reactivity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denis_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Denis-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The onset temperature of reaction between the basal plane of single-layer graphene and oxygen gas is below 260C (530K).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamada3_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yamada3-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene burns at very low temperature (e.g., 350C (620K)).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eftekhari_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eftekhari-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene is commonly modified with oxygen- and nitrogen-containing functional groups and analyzed by infrared spectroscopy and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy. However, determination of structures of graphene with oxygen-
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamada1_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yamada1-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and nitrogen-
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamada2_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yamada2-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      functional groups requires the structures to be well controlled.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2013,
      <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">
       Stanford University
      </a>
      physicists reported that single-layer graphene is a hundred times more chemically reactive than thicker sheets.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness">
         <span title="You can help  thicker sheets of what? Graphene is only one atom thick, so is the comparison with graphite? (June 2014)">
          vague
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Electronic">
       Electronic
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Electronic">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cnt_zz_v3.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="404" data-file-width="540" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Cnt_zz_v3.gif/350px-Cnt_zz_v3.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Cnt_zz_v3.gif/525px-Cnt_zz_v3.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/Cnt_zz_v3.gif 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cnt_zz_v3.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbon" title="Graphene nanoribbon">
         GNR
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Electronic_band_structure" title="Electronic band structure">
         band structure
        </a>
        for zig-zag orientation. Tightbinding calculations show that zigzag orientation is always metallic.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="404" data-file-width="540" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif/350px-Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif/525px-Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cnt_gnrarm_v3.gif" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbon" title="Graphene nanoribbon">
         GNR
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Electronic_band_structure" title="Electronic band structure">
         band structure
        </a>
        for armchair orientation. Tightbinding calculations show that armchair orientation can be semiconducting or metallic depending on width (chirality).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Graphene is a zero-gap
      <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">
       semiconductor
      </a>
      , because its
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conduction_band" title="Conduction band">
       conduction
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_bands" title="Valence bands">
       valence bands
      </a>
      meet at the Dirac points. The Dirac points are six locations in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Momentum_space" title="Momentum space">
       momentum space
      </a>
      , on the edge of the
      <a href="/wiki/Brillouin_zone" title="Brillouin zone">
       Brillouin zone
      </a>
      , divided into two non-equivalent sets of three points. The two sets are labeled K and K'. The sets give graphene a valley degeneracy of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <i>
        gv
       </i>
       = 2
      </span>
      . By contrast, for traditional semiconductors the primary point of interest is generally , where momentum is zero.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erog11_47-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-erog11-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Four electronic properties separate it from other
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Condensed_matter" title="Condensed matter">
       condensed matter
      </a>
      systems.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Electronic_spectrum">
       Electronic spectrum
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Electronic spectrum">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Electrons propagating through graphene's honeycomb lattice effectively lose their mass, producing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quasi-particle" title="Quasi-particle">
       quasi-particles
      </a>
      that are described by a 2D analogue of the
      <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">
       Dirac equation
      </a>
      rather than the
      <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrdinger equation">
       Schrdinger equation
      </a>
      for spin-
      <span class="frac nowrap">
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
      </span>
      particles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Castro-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E-Phonon_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-E-Phonon-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersion_relation">
       Dispersion relation
       <span id="pseudorel">
       </span>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Dispersion relation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      Using a conventional
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tight-binding" title="Tight-binding">
       tight-binding
      </a>
      model the
      <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_relation" title="Dispersion relation">
       dispersion relation
      </a>
      that produces energy of the electrons with wave vector
      <i>
       k
      </i>
      is
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Semenoff_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Semenoff-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallace_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wallace-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <img alt="E=\pm\sqrt{\gamma_0^2\left(1+4\cos^2{\frac{k_ya}{2}}+4\cos{\frac{k_ya}{2}} \cdot \cos{\frac{k_x\sqrt{3}a}{2}}\right)}" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/2/1/8/2187a8c2970de1aa45a97ddbffc23f35.png"/>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      with the nearest-neighbor hopping energy
      <i>
       
      </i>
      <sub>
       0
      </sub>
      
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6981448609416360000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        2.8
       </span>
       eV
      </span>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Lattice_constant" title="Lattice constant">
       lattice constant
      </a>
      <span class="nowrap">
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         6990246000000000000
        </span>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         2.46
        </span>
        
       </span>
      </span>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conduction_band" title="Conduction band">
       conduction
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_band" title="Valence band">
       valence bands
      </a>
      , respectively, correspond to the different signs. Two of the six Dirac points are independent, while the rest are equivalent by symmetry. In the vicinity of the K-points the energy depends
      <i>
       linearly
      </i>
      on the wave vector, similar to a relativistic particle.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Semenoff_65-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Semenoff-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBE_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CBE-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Since an elementary cell of the lattice has a basis of two atoms, the
      <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">
       wave function
      </a>
      has an effective
      <a href="/wiki/Spinor" title="Spinor">
       2-spinor structure
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      As a consequence, at low energies, even neglecting the true spin, the electrons can be described by an equation that is formally equivalent to the massless
      <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">
       Dirac equation
      </a>
      . Hence, the electrons and holes are called Dirac
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermions" title="Fermions">
       fermions
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Semenoff_65-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Semenoff-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This pseudo-relativistic description is restricted to the
      <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">
       chiral limit
      </a>
      , i.e., to vanishing rest mass
      <i>
       M
      </i>
      <sub>
       0
      </sub>
      , which leads to interesting additional features:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Semenoff_65-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Semenoff-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cabra2_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cabra2-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <img alt="v_F\, \vec \sigma \cdot \nabla \psi(\mathbf{r})\,=\,E\psi(\mathbf{r})." class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/a/3/1/a3123f50da39dbf1c3aa92e6cd5606e9.png"/>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Here
      <i>
       v
       <sub>
        F
       </sub>
      </i>
      ~
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7006100000000000000
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         6
        </span>
       </sup>
       m/s
      </span>
      (.003 c) is the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermi_velocity" title="Fermi velocity">
       Fermi velocity
      </a>
      in graphene, which replaces the velocity of light in the Dirac theory;
      <img alt="\vec{\sigma}" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/4/d/f4d5262a392d969a755d535329f3d0df.png"/>
      is the vector of the
      <a href="/wiki/Pauli_matrices" title="Pauli matrices">
       Pauli matrices
      </a>
      ,
      <img alt="\psi(\mathbf{r})" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/e/e/7/ee7e4804e3997f46feacec4aee210955.png"/>
      is the two-component wave function of the electrons, and
      <i>
       E
      </i>
      is their energy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Castro-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The equation describing the electrons' linear dispersion relation is
     </p>
     <p>
      <img alt="E=\hbar v_F\sqrt{k_x^2+k_y^2}" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/d/6/5/d65cc73fc84497a6bca1529feebe93be.png"/>
      ;
     </p>
     <p>
      where the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wavevector" title="Wavevector">
       wavevector
      </a>
      <i>
       k
      </i>
      is measured from the Dirac points (the zero of energy is chosen here to coincide with the Dirac points). The equation uses a pseudospin matrix formula that describes two sublattices of the honeycomb lattice.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CBE_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CBE-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Single-atom_wave_propagation">
       Single-atom wave propagation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Single-atom wave propagation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Electron waves in graphene propagate within a single-atom layer, making them sensitive to the proximity of other materials such as
      <a href="/wiki/High-%CE%BA_dielectric" title="High- dielectric">
       high- dielectrics
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor">
       superconductors
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferromagnetic" title="Ferromagnetic">
       ferromagnetics
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_transport">
       Electron transport
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Electron transport">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Experimental results from transport measurements show that graphene has a remarkable
      <a href="/wiki/Electron_mobility" title="Electron mobility">
       electron mobility
      </a>
      at room temperature, with reported values in excess of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7004150000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        15
        <span style="margin-left:.25em">
         000
        </span>
       </span>
       cm
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       V
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       s
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007_35-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Additionally, the symmetry of the experimentally measured conductance indicates that hole and electron mobilities should be nearly the same.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E-Phonon_64-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-E-Phonon-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The mobility is nearly independent of temperature between
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7001100000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        10
       </span>
       K
      </span>
      and
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7002100000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        100
       </span>
       K
      </span>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2dgasDiracFermions_26-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2dgasDiracFermions-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GiantMobility_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GiantMobility-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E-ph_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-E-ph-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which implies that the dominant scattering mechanism is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Defect_scattering" title="Defect scattering">
       defect scattering
      </a>
      . Scattering by the acoustic
      <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">
       phonons
      </a>
      of graphene intrinsically limits room temperature mobility to
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7005200000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        200
        <span style="margin-left:.25em">
         000
        </span>
       </span>
       cm
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       V
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       s
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      at a carrier density of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7012100000000000000
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         12
        </span>
       </sup>
       cm
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
      </span>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E-ph_70-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-E-ph-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrapheneMC_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GrapheneMC-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which was later demonstrated and is
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7007100000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        10
       </span>
       <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em">
        
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         6
        </span>
       </sup>
      </span>
      times greater than copper.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arx1406_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-arx1406-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The corresponding
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resistivity" title="Resistivity">
       resistivity
      </a>
      of the graphene sheet would be
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6994100000000000000
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         6
        </span>
       </sup>
       cm
      </span>
      . This is less than the resistivity of
      <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">
       silver
      </a>
      , the lowest known at room temperature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UMDnews_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-UMDnews-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, for room temperature graphene on
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      substrates, scattering of electrons by optical phonons of the substrate is a larger effect than scattering by graphenes own phonons. This limits mobility to
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7004400000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        40
        <span style="margin-left:.25em">
         000
        </span>
       </span>
       cm
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       V
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       s
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E-ph_70-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-E-ph-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Charge transport in graphene in ambient has major concerns due to adsorption of contamination such as water, oxygen, humid, etc. molecules. This leads to non-repetitive and large hysteresis I-V characteristics. Hence researchers carry out electrical measurements in vacuum. The protection of graphene surface by a coating with materials such as SiN, PMMA, h-BN, etc. have been discussed by researchers, but with limited success. In January 2015, first long term stable graphene device operation in air over several weeks has been reported by German researchers,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      where graphene surface is protected by aluminum oxide.
     </p>
     <p>
      Electrical resistance in 40-nanometer-wide
      <a href="/wiki/Nanoribbon" title="Nanoribbon">
       nanoribbons
      </a>
      of epitaxial graphene changes in discrete steps. The ribbons' conductance exceeds predictions by a factor of 10. The ribbons can act more like
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_waveguide" title="Optical waveguide">
       optical waveguides
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">
       quantum dots
      </a>
      , allowing electrons to flow smoothly along the ribbon edges. In copper, resistance increases in proportion to length as electrons encounter impurities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k1402_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-k1402-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi_12952_77-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-doi_12952-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Transport is dominated by two modes. One is ballistic and temperature independent, while the other is thermally activated. Ballistic electrons resemble those in cylindrical
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">
       carbon nanotubes
      </a>
      . At room temperature, resistance increases abruptly at a particular lengththe ballistic mode at 16 micrometres and the other at 160 nanometres (1% of the former length).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k1402_76-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-k1402-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The ribbons were grown on the edges of three-dimensional structures etched into
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">
       silicon carbide
      </a>
      wafers. When the wafers are heated to approximately 1,000C (1,270K; 1,830F), silicon is preferentially driven off along the edges, forming nanoribbons whose structure is determined by the pattern of the three-dimensional surface. The nanoribbons had perfectly smooth edges, annealed by the fabrication process. Electron mobility measurements surpassing one million correspond to a
      <a href="/wiki/Sheet_resistance" title="Sheet resistance">
       sheet resistance
      </a>
      of one ohm per square two orders of magnitude lower than in two-dimensional graphene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k1402_76-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-k1402-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene electrons can cover micrometer distances without scattering, even at room temperature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro_63-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Castro-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite zero carrier density near the Dirac points, graphene exhibits a minimum
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">
       conductivity
      </a>
      on the order of
      <img alt="4e^2/h" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/8/6/78628e8e665cc90c27ba0017a6ea88ab.png"/>
      . The origin of this minimum conductivity is still unclear. However, rippling of the graphene sheet or ionized impurities in the
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      substrate may lead to local puddles of carriers that allow conduction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E-Phonon_64-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-E-Phonon-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several theories suggest that the minimum conductivity should be
      <img alt="4e^2/{(\pi}h)" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/c/0/8/c08be9f6e07249a391cfee77c6918a30.png"/>
      ; however, most measurements are of order
      <img alt="4e^2/h" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/8/6/78628e8e665cc90c27ba0017a6ea88ab.png"/>
      or greater
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007_35-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and depend on impurity concentration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K_78-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-K-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Near zero carrier density graphene exhibits positive photoconductivity and negative photoconductivity at high carrier density. This is governed by the interplay between photoinduced changes of both the Drude weight and the carrier scattering rate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene doped with various gaseous species (both acceptors and donors) can be returned to an undoped state by gentle heating in vacuum.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K_78-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-K-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChemDoping_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ChemDoping-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even for
      <a href="/wiki/Dopant" title="Dopant">
       dopant
      </a>
      concentrations in excess of 10
      <sup>
       12
      </sup>
      cm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      carrier mobility exhibits no observable change.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChemDoping_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ChemDoping-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene doped with
      <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">
       potassium
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Ultra-high_vacuum" title="Ultra-high vacuum">
       ultra-high vacuum
      </a>
      at low temperature can reduce mobility 20-fold.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K_78-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-K-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrapheneCharge_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GrapheneCharge-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The mobility reduction is reversible on heating the graphene to remove the potassium.
     </p>
     <p>
      Due to graphene's two dimensions, charge fractionalization (where the apparent charge of individual pseudoparticles in low-dimensional systems is less than a single quantum
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ) is thought to occur. It may therefore be a suitable material for constructing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_computer" title="Quantum computer">
       quantum computers
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      using
      <a href="/wiki/Anyon" title="Anyon">
       anyonic
      </a>
      circuits.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
