       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anomalous_quantum_Hall_effect">
       Anomalous quantum Hall effect
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Anomalous quantum Hall effect">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-style ambox-technical" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          may be too
          <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/technical#Adjective" title="wikt:technical">
           technical
          </a>
          for most readers to understand
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please help
          <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit">
           improve
          </a>
          this section to
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Make_technical_articles_understandable" title="Wikipedia:Make technical articles understandable">
           make it understandable to non-experts
          </a>
          , without removing the technical details. The
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Graphene" title="Talk:Graphene">
           talk page
          </a>
          may contain suggestions.
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (December 2013)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Quantum_Hall_effect" title="Quantum Hall effect">
       quantum Hall effect
      </a>
      is a quantum mechanical version of the
      <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect" title="Hall effect">
       Hall effect
      </a>
      , which is the production of transverse (perpendicular to the main current) conductivity in the presence of a
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
       magnetic field
      </a>
      . The quantization of the
      <a href="/wiki/Hall_effect" title="Hall effect">
       Hall effect
      </a>
      <img alt="\sigma_{xy}" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/8/1/f/81f0b9926ae0f21415c48aaef6c32632.png"/>
      at integer multiples (the "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landau_level" title="Landau level">
       Landau level
      </a>
      ") of the basic quantity
      <img alt="e^2/h" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/5/1/1/5110cf9804cabadb89f408c64429e1c0.png"/>
      (where
      <i>
       e
      </i>
      is the elementary electric charge and
      <i>
       h
      </i>
      is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck's constant">
       Planck's constant
      </a>
      ) It can usually be observed only in very clean
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
       silicon
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Gallium_arsenide" title="Gallium arsenide">
       gallium arsenide
      </a>
      solids at temperatures around
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7000300000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        3
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
        K
       </a>
      </span>
      and very high magnetic fields.
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene shows the quantum Hall effect with respect to conductivity quantization: the effect is
      <i>
       anomalous
      </i>
      in that the sequence of steps is shifted by 1/2 with respect to the standard sequence and with an additional factor of 4. Graphene's Hall conductivity is
      <img alt="\sigma_{xy}=\pm {4\cdot\left(N + 1/2 \right)e^2}/h " class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/e/3/d/e3d932c61d667bf7071cd6ea69b39271.png"/>
      , where
      <i>
       N
      </i>
      is the Landau level and the double valley and double spin degeneracies give the factor of 4.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007_35-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These anomalies are present at room temperature, i.e. at roughly 20C (293K).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2dgasDiracFermions_26-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-2dgasDiracFermions-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      This behavior is a direct result of graphene's massless Dirac electrons. In a magnetic field, their spectrum has a Landau level with energy precisely at the Dirac point. This level is a consequence of the
      <a href="/wiki/Atiyah%E2%80%93Singer_index_theorem" title="AtiyahSinger index theorem">
       AtiyahSinger index theorem
      </a>
      and is half-filled in neutral graphene,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Semenoff_65-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Semenoff-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      leading to the "+1/2" in the Hall conductivity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gusynin_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gusynin-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Bilayer_graphene" title="Bilayer graphene">
       Bilayer graphene
      </a>
      also shows the quantum Hall effect, but with only one of the two anomalies (i.e.
      <img alt="\sigma_{xy}=\pm {4\cdot N\cdot e^2}/h " class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/1/b/b1b60f0efa491e6fe0f872acfc2a044d.png"/>
      ). In the second anomaly, the first plateau at
      <i>
       N=0
      </i>
      is absent, indicating that bilayer graphene stays metallic at the neutrality point.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007_35-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Unlike normal metals, graphene's longitudinal resistance shows maxima rather than minima for integral values of the Landau filling factor in measurements of the
      <a href="/wiki/Shubnikov%E2%80%93de_Haas_effect" title="Shubnikovde Haas effect">
       Shubnikovde Haas oscillations
      </a>
      , whereby the term
      <i>
       integral
      </i>
      quantum Hall effect. These oscillations show a phase shift of , known as
      <a href="/wiki/Geometric_phase" title="Geometric phase">
       Berrys phase
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2dgasDiracFermions_26-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-2dgasDiracFermions-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E-Phonon_64-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-E-Phonon-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Berrys phase arises due to the zero effective carrier mass near the Dirac points.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berry.27sPhase_28-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Berry.27sPhase-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The temperature dependence of the oscillations reveals that the carriers have a non-zero cyclotron mass, despite their zero effective mass.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2dgasDiracFermions_26-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-2dgasDiracFermions-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene samples prepared on nickel films, and on both the silicon face and carbon face of
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide#Structure_and_properties" title="Silicon carbide">
       silicon carbide
      </a>
      , show the anomalous effect directly in electrical measurements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ByungHeeHong_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ByungHeeHong-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-0908.1900_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-0908.1900-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShenAPL_87-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ShenAPL-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-0909.2903_88-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-0909.2903-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-0909.1193_89-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-0909.1193-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phase1_90-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-phase1-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphitic layers on the carbon face of silicon carbide show a clear
      <a href="/wiki/Dirac_spectrum" title="Dirac spectrum">
       Dirac spectrum
      </a>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ARPES" title="ARPES">
       angle-resolved photoemission
      </a>
      experiments, and the effect is observed in cyclotron resonance and tunneling experiments.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuhrer09_91-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fuhrer09-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_magnetic_fields">
       Strong magnetic fields
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Strong magnetic fields">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In magnetic fields above 10
      <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
       teslas
      </a>
      or so additional plateaus of the Hall conductivity at
      <span class="nowrap">
       <i>
        
       </i>
       <sub>
        <i>
         xy
        </i>
       </sub>
       =
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       /
       <i>
        h
       </i>
      </span>
      with
      <span class="nowrap">
       <i>
        
       </i>
       = 0, 1, 4
      </span>
      are observed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nu-0-1-4_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nu-0-1-4-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A plateau at
      <span class="nowrap">
       <i>
        
       </i>
       = 3
      </span>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nu-3_93-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nu-3-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Fractional_quantum_Hall_effect" title="Fractional quantum Hall effect">
       fractional quantum Hall effect
      </a>
      at
      <span class="nowrap">
       <i>
        
       </i>
       =
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        
        <sub>
         3
        </sub>
       </span>
      </span>
      were also reported.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nu-3_93-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nu-3-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nu-one3rd_94-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nu-one3rd-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      These observations with
      <span class="nowrap">
       <i>
        
       </i>
       = 0, 1, 3, 4
      </span>
      indicate that the four-fold degeneracy (two valley and two spin degrees of freedom) of the Landau energy levels is partially or completely lifted.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Casimir_effect">
       Casimir effect
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Casimir effect">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Casimir_effect" title="Casimir effect">
       Casimir effect
      </a>
      is an interaction between disjoint neutral bodies provoked by the fluctuations of the electrodynamical vacuum. Mathematically it can be explained by considering the normal modes of electromagnetic fields, which explicitly depend on the boundary (or matching) conditions on the interacting bodies' surfaces. Since graphene/electromagnetic field interaction is strong for a one-atom-thick material, the Casimir effect is of growing interest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFGV_95-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BFGV-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FMD_96-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FMD-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="van_der_Waals_force">
       van der Waals force
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: van der Waals force">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">
       van der Waals force
      </a>
      (or dispersion force) is also unusual, obeying an inverse cubic, asymptotic
      <a href="/wiki/Power_law" title="Power law">
       power law
      </a>
      in contrast to the usual inverse quartic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DWR_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DWR-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id=".22Massive.22_electrons">
       "Massive" electrons
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title='Edit section: "Massive" electrons'>
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Graphene's unit cell has two identical carbon atoms and two zero-energy states: one in which the electron resides on atom A, the other in which the electron resides on atom B. Both states exist at exactly zero energy. However, if the two atoms in the unit cell are not identical, the situation changes. Hunt et al. show that placing hBN in contact with graphene can alter the potential felt at atom A versus atom B enough that the electrons develop a mass and accompanying band gap of about 30 meV.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci1306_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sci1306-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The mass can be positive or negative. An arrangement that slightly raises the energy of an electron on atom A relative to atom B gives it a positive mass, while an arrangement that raises the energy of atom B produces a negative electron mass. The two versions behave alike and are indistinguishable via
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_spectroscopy" title="Optical spectroscopy">
       optical spectroscopy
      </a>
      . An electron traveling from a positive-mass region to a negative-mass region must cross an intermediate region where its mass once again becomes zero. This region is gapless and therefore metallic. Metallic modes bounding semiconducting regions of opposite-sign mass is a hallmark of a topological phase and display much the same physics as topological insulators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci1306_98-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-sci1306-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      If the mass in graphene can be controlled, electrons can be confined to massless regions by surrounding them with massive regions, allowing the patterning of
      <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dot" title="Quantum dot">
       quantum dots
      </a>
      , wires, and other mesoscopic structures. It also produces one-dimensional conductors along the boundary. These wires would be protected against
      <a href="/wiki/Backscatter" title="Backscatter">
       backscattering
      </a>
      and could carry currents without dissipation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci1306_98-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-sci1306-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Optical">
       Optical
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Optical">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:216px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graphene_visible.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="216" data-file-width="214" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/19/Graphene_visible.jpg" width="214"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graphene_visible.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <span id="photoopt">
        </span>
        Photograph of graphene in transmitted light. This one-atom-thick crystal can be seen with the naked eye because it absorbs approximately 2.6% of green light,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
         <a href="#cite_note-99">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          99
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        and 2.3% of red light.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nair_2008_100-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Nair_2008-100">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          100
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Graphene's unique optical properties produce an unexpectedly high
      <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">
       opacity
      </a>
      for an atomic monolayer in vacuum, absorbing
      <span class="nowrap">
       <i>
        
       </i>
        2.3%
      </span>
      of red
      <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">
       light
      </a>
      , where
      <i>
       
      </i>
      is the
      <a href="/wiki/Fine-structure_constant" title="Fine-structure constant">
       fine-structure constant
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This is a consequence of the "unusual low-energy electronic structure of monolayer graphene that features electron and hole
      <a href="/wiki/Conical_intersection" title="Conical intersection">
       conical bands
      </a>
      meeting each other at the
      <a href="/wiki/Graphene#Electronic_properties" title="Graphene">
       Dirac point
      </a>
      ... [which] is qualitatively different from more common
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Quadratic_massive_band&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Quadratic massive band (page does not exist)">
       quadratic massive bands
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nair_2008_100-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nair_2008-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Based on the SlonczewskiWeissMcClure (SWMcC) band model of graphite, the interatomic distance, hopping value and frequency cancel when optical conductance is calculated using
      <a href="/wiki/Fresnel_equations" title="Fresnel equations">
       Fresnel equations
      </a>
      in the thin-film limit.
     </p>
     <p>
      Although confirmed experimentally, the measurement is not precise enough to improve on other techniques for determining the
      <a href="/wiki/Fine-structure_constant" title="Fine-structure constant">
       fine-structure constant
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene's
      <a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">
       band gap
      </a>
      can be tuned from 0 to
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6980400544121749999
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        0.25
       </span>
       eV
      </span>
      (about 5 micrometre wavelength) by applying voltage to a dual-gate
      <a href="/wiki/Bilayer_graphene" title="Bilayer graphene">
       bilayer graphene
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Field-effect_transistor" title="Field-effect transistor">
       field-effect transistor
      </a>
      (FET) at room temperature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The optical response of
      <a href="/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbons" title="Graphene nanoribbons">
       graphene nanoribbons
      </a>
      is tunable into the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terahertz" title="Terahertz">
       terahertz
      </a>
      regime by an applied magnetic field.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene/graphene oxide systems exhibit
      <a href="/wiki/Graphene#Electrochromic_devices" title="Graphene">
       electrochromic behavior
      </a>
      , allowing tuning of both linear and ultrafast optical properties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kurum2011_105-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kurum2011-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A graphene-based
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bragg_grating" title="Bragg grating">
       Bragg grating
      </a>
      (one-dimensional
      <a href="/wiki/Photonic_crystal" title="Photonic crystal">
       photonic crystal
      </a>
      ) has been fabricated and demonstrated its capability for excitation of surface electromagnetic waves in the periodic structure by using
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6993633000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        633
       </span>
       nm
      </span>
      HeNe laser as the light source.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Saturable_absorption">
       Saturable absorption
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Saturable absorption">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Such unique absorption could become saturated when the input optical intensity is above a threshold value. This nonlinear optical behavior is termed
      <a href="/wiki/Saturable_absorption" title="Saturable absorption">
       saturable absorption
      </a>
      and the threshold value is called the saturation fluence. Graphene can be saturated readily under strong excitation over the visible to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">
       near-infrared
      </a>
      region, due to the universal optical absorption and zero band gap. This has relevance for the mode locking of
      <a href="/wiki/Fiber_laser" title="Fiber laser">
       fiber lasers
      </a>
      , where fullband mode locking has been achieved by graphene-based saturable absorber. Due to this special property, graphene has wide application in ultrafast
      <a href="/wiki/Photonics" title="Photonics">
       photonics
      </a>
      . Moreover, the optical response of graphene/graphene oxide layers can be tuned electrically.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kurum2011_105-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kurum2011-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Saturable absorption in graphene could occur at the Microwave and Terahertz band, owing to its wideband optical absorption property. The microwave saturable absorption in graphene demonstrates the possibility of graphene microwave and terahertz photonics devices, such as microwave saturable absorber, modulator, polarizer, microwave signal processing and broad-band wireless access networks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zheng_108-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Zheng-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nonlinear_Kerr_effect">
       Nonlinear Kerr effect
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nonlinear Kerr effect">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Under more intensive laser illumination, graphene could also possess a nonlinear phase shift due to the optical nonlinear
      <a href="/wiki/Kerr_effect" title="Kerr effect">
       Kerr effect
      </a>
      . Based on a typical open and close aperture z-scan measurement, graphene possesses a giant non-linear Kerr coefficient of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6993100000000000000
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         7
        </span>
       </sup>
       cm
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       W
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      , almost nine orders of magnitude larger than that of bulk dielectrics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZHANGHAN_109-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ZHANGHAN-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This suggests that graphene may be a powerful nonlinear Kerr medium, with the possibility of observing a variety of nonlinear effects, the most important of which is the
      <a href="/wiki/Soliton" title="Soliton">
       soliton
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Excitonic">
       Excitonic
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Excitonic">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      First-principle calculations with quasiparticle corrections and many-body effects are performed to study the electronic and optical properties of graphene-based materials. The approach is described as three stages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With GW calculation, the properties of graphene-based materials are accurately investigated, including graphene,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbons" title="Graphene nanoribbons">
       graphene nanoribbons
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      edge and surface functionalized armchair graphene nanoribbons,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      hydrogen saturated armchair graphene nanoribbons,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Josephson_effect" title="Josephson effect">
       Josephson effect
      </a>
      in graphene SNS junctions with single localized defect
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and scaling properties in armchair graphene nanoribbons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Stability">
       Stability
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Stability">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ab_initio_quantum_chemistry_methods" title="Ab initio quantum chemistry methods">
       Ab initio calculations
      </a>
      show that a graphene sheet is thermodynamically unstable if its size is less than about
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6992200000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        20
       </span>
       nm
      </span>
      ("graphene is the least stable structure until about 6000 atoms") and becomes the most stable
      <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">
       fullerene
      </a>
      (as within graphite) only for molecules larger than 24,000 atoms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stability_118-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-stability-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thermal_conductivity">
       Thermal conductivity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Thermal conductivity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Thermal transport in graphene is an active area of research, which has attracted attention because of the potential for thermal management applications. Early measurements of the
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">
       thermal conductivity
      </a>
      of suspended graphene reported an exceptionally large thermal conductivity of approximately
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7003530000000000000
       </span>
       5300Wm
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       K
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balandin_119-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Balandin-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      compared with the thermal conductivity of pyrolytic
      <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">
       graphite
      </a>
      of approximately
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7003200000000000000
       </span>
       2000Wm
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       K
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      at room temperature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Touloukian1970_120-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Touloukian1970-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, later studies have questioned whether this ultrahigh value had been overestimated, and have instead measured a wide range of thermal conductivities between
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7003150000000000000
       </span>
       1500
      </span>
      
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7003250000000000000
       </span>
       2500Wm
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       K
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      for suspended single layer graphene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CaiMoore2010_121-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CaiMoore2010-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FaugerasFaugeras2010_122-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FaugerasFaugeras2010-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XuPereira2014_123-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-XuPereira2014-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeYoon2011_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LeeYoon2011-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The large range in the reported thermal conductivity can be caused by large measurement uncertainties as well as variations in the graphene quality and processing conditions. In addition, it is known that when single-layer graphene is supported on an amorphous material, the thermal conductivity is reduced to about
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7002500000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        500
       </span>
      </span>
      
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7002600000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        600
       </span>
       Wm
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       K
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      at room temperature as a result of scattering of graphene lattice waves by the substrate,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeolJo2010_125-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SeolJo2010-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klemens2001_126-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Klemens2001-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and can be even lower for few layer graphene encased in amorphous oxide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JangChen2010_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JangChen2010-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Likewise, polymeric residue can contribute to a similar decrease in the thermal conductivity of suspended graphene to approximately
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7002500000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        500
       </span>
      </span>
      
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7002600000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        600
       </span>
       Wm
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       K
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      for bilayer graphene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PettesJo2011_128-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PettesJo2011-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It has been suggested that the isotopic composition, the ratio of
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">
       <sup>
        12
       </sup>
       C
      </a>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13">
       <sup>
        13
       </sup>
       C
      </a>
      , has a significant impact on the thermal conductivity. For example, isotopically pure
      <sup>
       12
      </sup>
      C graphene has higher thermal conductivity than either a 50:50 isotope ratio or the naturally occurring 99:1 ratio.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chen2012natmat_129-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-chen2012natmat-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It can be shown by using the
      <a href="/wiki/Wiedemann%E2%80%93Franz_law" title="WiedemannFranz law">
       WiedemannFranz law
      </a>
      , that the thermal conduction is
      <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">
       phonon
      </a>
      -dominated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balandin_119-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Balandin-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, for a gated graphene strip, an applied gate bias causing a
      <a href="/wiki/Fermi_energy" title="Fermi energy">
       Fermi energy
      </a>
      shift much larger than
      <i>
       k
      </i>
      <sub>
       B
      </sub>
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      can cause the electronic contribution to increase and dominate over the
      <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">
       phonon
      </a>
      contribution at low temperatures. The ballistic thermal conductance of graphene is isotropic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saito_130-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Saito-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qizhen_Liang.2C_Xuxia_Yao.2C_Wei_Wang.2C_Yan_Liu.2C_Ching_Ping_Wong._2011_2392.E2.80.932401_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Qizhen_Liang.2C_Xuxia_Yao.2C_Wei_Wang.2C_Yan_Liu.2C_Ching_Ping_Wong._2011_2392.E2.80.932401-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Potential for this high conductivity can be seen by considering graphite, a 3D version of graphene that has
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basal_plane" title="Basal plane">
       basal plane
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">
       thermal conductivity
      </a>
      of over a
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7003100000000000000
       </span>
       1000Wm
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
       K
       <sup>
        1
       </sup>
      </span>
      (comparable to
      <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">
       diamond
      </a>
      ). In graphite, the c-axis (out of plane) thermal conductivity is over a factor of ~100 smaller due to the weak binding forces between basal planes as well as the larger
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lattice_spacing" title="Lattice spacing">
       lattice spacing
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition, the ballistic thermal conductance of graphene is shown to give the lower limit of the ballistic thermal conductances, per unit circumference, length of carbon nanotubes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mingo_133-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mingo-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite its 2-D nature, graphene has 3
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acoustic_phonon" title="Acoustic phonon">
       acoustic phonon
      </a>
      modes. The two in-plane modes (LA, TA) have a linear
      <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_relation" title="Dispersion relation">
       dispersion relation
      </a>
      , whereas the out of plane mode (ZA) has a quadratic dispersion relation. Due to this, the
      <i>
       T
      </i>
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      dependent thermal conductivity contribution of the linear modes is dominated at low temperatures by the T
      <sup>
       1.5
      </sup>
      contribution of the out of plane mode.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mingo_133-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-mingo-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some graphene phonon bands display negative
      <a href="/wiki/Gr%C3%BCneisen_parameter" title="Grneisen parameter">
       Grneisen parameters
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mounet_134-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mounet-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At low temperatures (where most optical modes with positive Grneisen parameters are still not excited) the contribution from the negative Grneisen parameters will be dominant and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion_coefficient" title="Thermal expansion coefficient">
       thermal expansion coefficient
      </a>
      (which is directly proportional to Grneisen parameters) negative. The lowest negative Grneisen parameters correspond to the lowest transverse acoustic ZA modes. Phonon frequencies for such modes increase with the in-plane
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lattice_parameter" title="Lattice parameter">
       lattice parameter
      </a>
      since atoms in the layer upon stretching will be less free to move in the z direction. This is similar to the behavior of a string, which, when it is stretched, will have vibrations of smaller amplitude and higher frequency. This phenomenon, named "membrane effect," was predicted by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilya_Mikhailovich_Lifshitz" title="Ilya Mikhailovich Lifshitz">
       Lifshitz
      </a>
      in 1952.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lifshitz_135-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lifshitz-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical">
       Mechanical
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mechanical">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93carbon_bond" title="Carboncarbon bond">
       carboncarbon bond
      </a>
      length in graphene is about 0.142
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanometer" title="Nanometer">
       nanometers
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene sheets stack to form graphite with an interplanar spacing of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6990335000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        0.335
       </span>
       nm
      </span>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene is the strongest material ever tested,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sci_137-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sci-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with an intrinsic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">
       Tensile strength
      </a>
      of 130 GPa and a
      <a href="/wiki/Young%27s_modulus" title="Young's modulus">
       Young's modulus
      </a>
      (stiffness) of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7000100000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        1
       </span>
       TPa
      </span>
      (
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7008150000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        150
        <span style="margin-left:.25em">
         000
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em">
         000
        </span>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">
        psi
       </a>
      </span>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee_138-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lee-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Nobel announcement illustrated this by saying that a 1 square meter graphene hammock would support a
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7000400000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        4
       </span>
       kg
      </span>
      cat but would weigh only as much as one of the cat's whiskers, at
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6993770009999999999
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        0.77
       </span>
       mg
      </span>
      (about 0.001% of the weight of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7000100000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        1
       </span>
       m
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
      </span>
      of paper).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobelprize.org_139-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nobelprize.org-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      I
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_constant" title="Spring constant">
       spring constant
      </a>
      of suspended graphene sheets has been measured using an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_force_microscope" title="Atomic force microscope">
       atomic force microscope
      </a>
      (AFM). Graphene sheets were suspended over
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       SiO
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      cavities where an AFM tip was used to apply a stress to the sheet to test its mechanical properties. Its spring constant was in the range 15N/m and the stiffness was
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6999500000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        0.5
       </span>
       TPa
      </span>
      , which differs from that of bulk graphite. These intrinsic properties could lead to applications such as
      <a href="/wiki/Nanoelectromechanical_systems" title="Nanoelectromechanical systems">
       NEMS
      </a>
      as pressure sensors and resonators.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Due to its large surface energy and out of plane ductility, flat graphene sheets are unstable with respect to scrolling, i.e. bending into a cylindrical shape, which is its lower-energy state.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nmscrolling_141-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nmscrolling-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As is true of all materials, regions of graphene are subject to thermal and quantum fluctuations in relative displacement. Although the amplitude of these fluctuations is bounded in 3D structures (even in the limit of infinite size), the
      <a href="/wiki/Mermin%E2%80%93Wagner_theorem" title="MerminWagner theorem">
       MerminWagner theorem
      </a>
      shows that the amplitude of long-wavelength fluctuations grows logarithmically with the scale of a 2D structure, and would therefore be unbounded in structures of infinite size. Local deformation and elastic strain are negligibly affected by this long-range divergence in relative displacement. It is believed that a sufficiently large 2D structure, in the absence of applied lateral tension, will bend and crumple to form a fluctuating 3D structure. Researchers have observed ripples in suspended layers of graphene,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer07_30-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Meyer07-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and it has been proposed that the ripples are caused by thermal fluctuations in the material. As a consequence of these dynamical deformations, it is debatable whether graphene is truly a 2D structure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007_35-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeimNovoselov2007-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlsson_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Carlsson-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fasolino_54-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fasolino-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has recently been shown that these ripples, if amplified through the introduction of vacancy defects, can impart a negative
      <a href="/wiki/Poisson%27s_ratio" title="Poisson's ratio">
       Poisson's ratio
      </a>
      into graphene, resulting in the thinnest
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxetic" title="Auxetic">
       auxetic
      </a>
      material known so far.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fracture_Toughness">
       Fracture Toughness
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Fracture Toughness">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In 2014, researchers from
      <a href="/wiki/Rice_University" title="Rice University">
       Rice University
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Institute_of_Technology" title="Georgia Institute of Technology">
       Georgia Institute of Technology
      </a>
      have indicated that despite its strength, graphene is also relatively brittle, with a fracture toughness of ~4 MPam.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZhangMa2014_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ZhangMa2014-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This indicates that imperfect graphene is likely to crack in a brittle manner like
      <a href="/wiki/Ceramic_materials" title="Ceramic materials">
       ceramic materials
      </a>
      , as opposed to many metallic materials which tend to have fracture toughnesses in the range of 1550MPam. Later in 2014, the Rice team announced that graphene showed a greater ability to distribute force from an impact than any known material, 10x that of steel per unit weight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The force was transmitted at 22.2 kilometres per second (13.8mi/s).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spin_transport">
       Spin transport
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Spin transport">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Graphene is claimed to be an ideal material for
      <a href="/wiki/Spintronics" title="Spintronics">
       spintronics
      </a>
      due to its small
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-orbit_interaction" title="Spin-orbit interaction">
       spin-orbit interaction
      </a>
      and the near absence of
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_moment" title="Nuclear magnetic moment">
       nuclear magnetic moments
      </a>
      in carbon (as well as a weak
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperfine_interaction" title="Hyperfine interaction">
       hyperfine interaction
      </a>
      ). Electrical
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin_current" title="Spin current">
       spin current
      </a>
      injection and detection has been demonstrated up to room temperature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tombros_147-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tombros-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChoSpin_148-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ChoSpin-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ohishi_149-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ohishi-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Spin coherence length above 1 micrometre at room temperature was observed,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tombros_147-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tombros-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and control of the spin current polarity with an electrical gate was observed at low temperature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChoSpin_148-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ChoSpin-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Strong_magnetic_fields_2">
       Strong magnetic fields
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Strong magnetic fields">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Graphene's quantum Hall effect in magnetic fields above 10
      <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">
       Teslas
      </a>
      or so reveals additional interesting features. Additional plateaus of the Hall conductivity at
      <img alt="\sigma_{xy}=\nu e^2/h" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/a/0/7a049491f8dc9cf7817302068c02af49.png"/>
      with
      <img alt="\nu=0,\pm {1},\pm {4}" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/0/9/8/09813dd088151e696a078c1a0b1d765b.png"/>
      are observed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nu-0-1-4_92-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nu-0-1-4-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also, the observation of a plateau at
      <img alt="\nu=3" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/d/9/b/d9b0cbdd8bffb12a3ce7c724858fd230.png"/>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nu-3_93-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-nu-3-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the fractional quantum Hall effect at
      <img alt="\nu=1/3" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/1/3/7/137268e6d3d866e4c7b0b6017fcb1cd9.png"/>
      were reported.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nu-3_93-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-nu-3-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nu-one3rd_94-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nu-one3rd-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      These observations with
      <img alt="\nu=0,\pm 1,\pm 3, \pm 4" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/8/5/b8599c7c82cf3f5cf2d10030fc43b1e8.png"/>
      indicate that the four-fold degeneracy (two valley and two spin degrees of freedom) of the Landau energy levels is partially or completely lifted. One hypothesis is that the
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_catalysis" title="Magnetic catalysis">
       magnetic catalysis
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Symmetry_breaking" title="Symmetry breaking">
       symmetry breaking
      </a>
      is responsible for lifting the degeneracy.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Spintronic and magnetic properties can be present in graphene simultaneously.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Low-defect graphene nanomeshes manufactured by using a non-lithographic method exhibit large-amplitude ferromagnetism even at room temperature. Additionally a spin pumping effect is found for fields applied in parallel with the planes of few-layer ferromagnetic nanomeshes, while a magnetoresistance hysteresis loop is observed under perpendicular fields.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic">
       Magnetic
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Magnetic">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2014 researchers magnetized graphene by placing it on an atomically smooth layer of magnetic
      <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_iron_garnet" title="Yttrium iron garnet">
       yttrium iron garnet
      </a>
      . The graphene's electronic properties were unaffected. Prior approaches involved doping graphene with other substances. The dopant's presence negatively affected its electronic properties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
       <a href="#cite_note-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biological">
       Biological
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Biological">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2015 researchers used graphene to create sensitive biosensors by using epitaxial graphene on silicon carbide. The sensors bind to the 8-hydroxydeoxyguanosine (8-OHdG) and is capable of selective binding with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">
       antibodies
      </a>
      . The presence of 8-OHdG in blood, urine and saliva is commonly associated with DNA damage. Elevated levels of 8-OHdG have been linked to increased risk of developing several cancers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Forms">
       Forms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Forms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Monolayer_sheets">
       Monolayer sheets
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Monolayer sheets">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2013 a group of Polish scientists have presented a production unit that allows to manufacture continuous monolayer sheets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
       <a href="#cite_note-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The process is based on graphene growth on a liquid metal matrix.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The product of this process was called
      <a href="/wiki/HSMG" title="HSMG">
       HSMG
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bilayer">
       Bilayer
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Bilayer">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Bilayer_graphene" title="Bilayer graphene">
       Bilayer graphene
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Bilayer graphene displays the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anomalous_quantum_Hall_effect" title="Anomalous quantum Hall effect">
       anomalous quantum Hall effect
      </a>
      , a tunable
      <a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">
       band gap
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRB75.155115_155-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PRB75.155115-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and potential for
      <a href="/wiki/Exciton#Interaction" title="Exciton">
       excitonic condensation
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRL104.096802_156-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PRL104.096802-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      making it a promising candidate for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optoelectronic" title="Optoelectronic">
       optoelectronic
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanoelectronic" title="Nanoelectronic">
       nanoelectronic
      </a>
      applications. Bilayer graphene typically can be found either in twisted configurations where the two layers are rotated relative to each other or graphitic Bernal stacked configurations where half the atoms in one layer lie atop half the atoms in the other. Stacking order and orientation govern the optical and electronic properties of bilayer graphene.
     </p>
     <p>
      One way to synthesize bilayer graphene is via
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition" title="Chemical vapor deposition">
       chemical vapor deposition
      </a>
      , which can produce large bilayer regions that almost exclusively conform to a Bernal stack geometry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nl204547v_157-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nl204547v-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nanostripes">
       Nanostripes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Nanostripes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Graphene_nanoribbons" title="Graphene nanoribbons">
       Graphene nanoribbons
      </a>
      ("nanostripes" in the "zig-zag" orientation), at low temperatures, show spin-polarized metallic edge currents, which also suggests applications in the new field of
      <a href="/wiki/Spintronics" title="Spintronics">
       spintronics
      </a>
      . (In the "armchair" orientation, the edges behave like semiconductors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro_63-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Castro-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      )
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oxide">
       Oxide
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Oxide">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Graphite_oxide" title="Graphite oxide">
       Graphite oxide
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Using paper-making techniques on dispersed, oxidized and chemically processed graphite in water, the monolayer flakes form a single sheet and create strong bonds. These sheets, called
      <a href="/wiki/Graphene_oxide_paper" title="Graphene oxide paper">
       graphene oxide paper
      </a>
      have a measured
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_Modulus" title="Tensile Modulus">
       tensile modulus
      </a>
      of 32
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPa" title="GPa">
       GPa
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
       <a href="#cite_note-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The chemical property of graphite oxide is related to the functional groups attached to graphene sheets. These can change the polymerization pathway and similar chemical processes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
       <a href="#cite_note-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene oxide flakes in polymers display enhanced photo-conducting properties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
       <a href="#cite_note-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene is normally hydrophobic and impermeable to all gases and liquids (vacuum-tight). However when formed into graphene oxide-based capillary membrane, both liquid water and water vapor flow through as quickly as if the membrane was not present.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22282806_161-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid22282806-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_modification">
       Chemical modification
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Chemical modification">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-style ambox-technical" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section
         <b>
          may be too
          <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/technical#Adjective" title="wikt:technical">
           technical
          </a>
          for most readers to understand
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Please help
          <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit">
           improve
          </a>
          this section to
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Make_technical_articles_understandable" title="Wikipedia:Make technical articles understandable">
           make it understandable to non-experts
          </a>
          , without removing the technical details. The
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Graphene" title="Talk:Graphene">
           talk page
          </a>
          may contain suggestions.
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (December 2013)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slgo.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="216" data-file-width="960" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/70/Slgo.jpg/350px-Slgo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/70/Slgo.jpg/525px-Slgo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/70/Slgo.jpg/700px-Slgo.jpg 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slgo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Photograph of single-layer graphene oxide undergoing high temperature chemical treatment, resulting in sheet folding and loss of carboxylic functionality, or through room temperature carbodiimide treatment, collapsing into star-like clusters.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Soluble fragments of graphene can be prepared in the laboratory
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
       <a href="#cite_note-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      through chemical modification of graphite. First, microcrystalline graphite is treated with an acidic mixture of sulfuric acid and
      <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">
       nitric acid
      </a>
      . A series of oxidation and exfoliation steps produce small graphene plates with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboxyl" title="Carboxyl">
       carboxyl
      </a>
      groups at their edges. These are converted to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid_chloride" title="Acid chloride">
       acid chloride
      </a>
      groups by treatment with
      <a href="/wiki/Thionyl_chloride" title="Thionyl chloride">
       thionyl chloride
      </a>
      ; next, they are converted to the corresponding graphene
      <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">
       amide
      </a>
      via treatment with
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Octadecylamine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Octadecylamine (page does not exist)">
       octadecylamine
      </a>
      . The resulting material (circular graphene layers of 5.3
      <a href="/wiki/Angstrom" title="Angstrom">
       angstrom
      </a>
      thickness) is soluble in
      <a href="/wiki/Tetrahydrofuran" title="Tetrahydrofuran">
       tetrahydrofuran
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetrachloromethane" title="Tetrachloromethane">
       tetrachloromethane
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/1,2-Dichloroethane" title="1,2-Dichloroethane">
       dichloroethane
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Refluxing single-layer graphene oxide (SLGO) in
      <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">
       solvents
      </a>
