      leads to size reduction and folding of individual sheets as well as loss of carboxylic group functionality, by up to 20%, indicating thermal instabilities of SLGO sheets dependent on their preparation methodology. When using thionyl chloride,
      <a href="/wiki/Acyl_chloride" title="Acyl chloride">
       acyl chloride
      </a>
      groups result, which can then form aliphatic and aromatic amides with a reactivity conversion of around 7080%.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graphene_chemistry.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="452" data-file-width="827" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5f/Graphene_chemistry.jpg/350px-Graphene_chemistry.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5f/Graphene_chemistry.jpg/525px-Graphene_chemistry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5f/Graphene_chemistry.jpg/700px-Graphene_chemistry.jpg 2x" width="350"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graphene_chemistry.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Boehm titration results for various chemical reactions of single-layer graphene oxide, which reveal reactivity of the carboxylic groups and the resultant stability of the SLGO sheets after treatment.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">
       Hydrazine
      </a>
      reflux is commonly used for reducing SLGO to SLG(R), but
      <a href="/wiki/Titration" title="Titration">
       titrations
      </a>
      show that only around 2030% of the carboxylic groups are lost, leaving a significant number available for chemical attachment. Analysis of SLG(R) generated by this route reveals that the system is unstable and using a room temperature stirring with HCl (&lt; 1.0 M) leads to around 60% loss of COOH functionality. Room temperature treatment of SLGO with
      <a href="/wiki/Carbodiimide" title="Carbodiimide">
       carbodiimides
      </a>
      leads to the collapse of the individual sheets into star-like clusters that exhibited poor subsequent reactivity with amines (ca. 35% conversion of the intermediate to the final amide).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
       <a href="#cite_note-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is apparent that conventional chemical treatment of carboxylic groups on SLGO generates morphological changes of individual sheets that leads to a reduction in chemical reactivity, which may potentially limit their use in composite synthesis. Therefore, chemical reactions types have been explored. SLGO has also been grafted with
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Polyallylamine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Polyallylamine (page does not exist)">
       polyallylamine
      </a>
      , cross-linked through
      <a href="/wiki/Epoxy" title="Epoxy">
       epoxy
      </a>
      groups. When filtered into graphene oxide paper, these composites exhibit increased stiffness and strength relative to unmodified graphene oxide paper.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
       <a href="#cite_note-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Full
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">
       hydrogenation
      </a>
      from both sides of graphene sheet results in
      <a href="/wiki/Graphane" title="Graphane">
       graphane
      </a>
      , but partial hydrogenation leads to hydrogenated graphene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
       <a href="#cite_note-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Similarly, both-side fluorination of graphene (or chemical and mechanical exfoliation of graphite fluoride) leads to
      <a href="/wiki/Fluorographene" title="Fluorographene">
       fluorographene
      </a>
      (graphene fluoride),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while partial fluorination (generally halogenation) provides fluorinated (halogenated) graphene.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ligand.2F_Complex">
       Ligand/ Complex
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Ligand/ Complex">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Graphene can be a huge ligand to coordinate metals and metal ions by introducing functional groups. Structures of graphene ligands are similar to e.g. metal-
      <a href="/wiki/Porphyrin" title="Porphyrin">
       porphyrin
      </a>
      complex, metal-
      <a href="/wiki/Phthalocyanine" title="Phthalocyanine">
       phthalocyanine
      </a>
      complex, and metal-
      <a href="/wiki/Phenanthroline" title="Phenanthroline">
       phenanthroline
      </a>
      complex. Copper and nickel ions has been coordinated with graphene ligands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fiber">
       Fiber
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Fiber">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2011, Xinming Li and Hongwei Zhu from Tsinghua University reported a novel yet simple approach to fabricate graphene fibers from chemical vapor deposition grown graphene films.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The method was scalable and controllable, delivering tunable morphology and pore structure by controlling the evaporation of solvents with suitable surface tension. Flexible all-solid-state supercapacitors based on this graphene fibers were demonstrated in 2013.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
       <a href="#cite_note-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="3D">
       3D
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: 3D">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2013, a three-dimensional
      <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb" title="Honeycomb">
       honeycomb
      </a>
      of hexagonally arranged carbon was termed 3D graphene, although self-supporting 3D graphene has not yet been produced.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
       <a href="#cite_note-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However 3D
      <i>
       chemically modified graphene
      </i>
      is a self-supporting material that is characterised as ultralight cellular network ( &gt;1mg/cm).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
       <a href="#cite_note-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Three dimensional bilayer graphene has been reported.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reinforced">
       Reinforced
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Reinforced">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Graphene reinforced with embedded
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">
       carbon nanotube
      </a>
      reinforcing bars (
      <a href="/wiki/Rebar" title="Rebar">
       rebar
      </a>
      ) is easier to manipulate, while improving the electrical and mechanical qualities of both materials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurz5011_174-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kurz5011-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Functionalized single- or multiwalled carbon nanotubes are spin-coated on copper foils and then heated and cooled, using the nanotubes themselves as the carbon source. Under heating, the functional
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_group" title="Carbon group">
       carbon groups
      </a>
      decompose into graphene, while the nanotubes partially split and form in-plane
      <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">
       covalent bonds
      </a>
      with the graphene, adding strength.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pi_stacking" title="Pi stacking">
        stacking
      </a>
      domains add more strength. The nanotubes can overlap, making the material a better conductor than standard CVD-grown graphene. The nanotubes effectively bridge the
      <a href="/wiki/Grain_boundary" title="Grain boundary">
       grain boundaries
      </a>
      found in conventional graphene. The technique eliminates the traces of substrate on which later-separated sheets were deposited using epitaxy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurz5011_174-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-kurz5011-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Stacks of a few layers, have been proposed as a cost-effective and physically flexible replacement for
      <a href="/wiki/Indium_tin_oxide" title="Indium tin oxide">
       indium tin oxide
      </a>
      (ITO) used in displays and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_cell" title="Photovoltaic cell">
       photovoltaic cells
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurz5011_174-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-kurz5011-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Production_techniques">
       Production techniques
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Production techniques">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Isolated 2D crystals cannot be grown via chemical synthesis beyond small sizes even in principle, because the rapid growth of phonon density with increasing lateral size forces 2D crystallites to bend into the third dimension.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sciencerev09_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sciencerev09-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, other routes to 2d materials exist:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       Fundamental forces place seemingly insurmountable barriers in the way of creating [2D crystals]... The nascent 2D crystallites try to minimize their surface energy and inevitably morph into one of the rich variety of stable 3D structures that occur in soot.
      </p>
      <p>
       But there is a way around the problem. Interactions with 3D structures stabilize 2D crystals during growth. So one can make 2D crystals sandwiched between or placed on top of the atomic planes of a bulk crystal. In that respect, graphene already exists within graphite... One can then hope to fool Nature and extract single-atom-thick crystallites at a low enough temperature that they remain in the quenched state prescribed by the original higher-temperature 3D growth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysTod_176-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PhysTod-176">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         176
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      An expanding set of production techniques has supplemented the early approaches of cleaving multi-layer graphite into single layers or to grow it epitaxially by depositing a layer of carbon onto another material. In all cases, the graphite must bond to some substrate to retain its 2d shape.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sciencerev09_17-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sciencerev09-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Exfoliation">
       Exfoliation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Exfoliation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      As of 2014 exfoliation produced graphene with the lowest number of defects and highest electron mobility.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arx1406_72-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-arx1406-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Adhesive_tape">
       Adhesive tape
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Adhesive tape">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Cleavage is also known as
      <a href="/wiki/Exfoliation_joint" title="Exfoliation joint">
       exfoliation
      </a>
      . Achieving single layers typically requires multiple exfoliation steps, each producing a slice with fewer layers, until only one remains. Geim and Novoselov used
      <a href="/wiki/Adhesive_tape" title="Adhesive tape">
       adhesive tape
      </a>
      to split their graphene. Residues from the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Adhesive_tape" title="Adhesive tape">
       adhesive tape
      </a>
      might contaminate the graphene flake.
     </p>
     <p>
      After exfoliation the flakes are deposited on a silicon wafer using "dry deposition." Crystallites larger than 1mm and visible to the naked eye can be obtained with the technique. It is often referred to as a "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scotch_tape" title="Scotch tape">
       scotch tape
      </a>
      " or "drawing" method. The latter name appeared because the dry deposition resembles drawing with a piece of graphite.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysTod_176-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-PhysTod-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Wedge-based_mechanical_exfoliation">
       Wedge-based mechanical exfoliation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Wedge-based mechanical exfoliation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Another controlled technique to produce few layers of graphene uses a wedge type of tool
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jayasena2011_177-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jayasena2011-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to address difficulties of the adhesive tape method. In this method, a
      <a href="/wiki/Wedge_based_mechanical_exfoliation" title="Wedge based mechanical exfoliation">
       sharp single-crystal diamond wedge
      </a>
      penetrates onto the graphite source to exfoliate layers. This method uses highly ordered pyrolytic graphite (HOPG) as the starting material. The experiments were supported by molecular dynamic simulations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaya2013_178-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jaya2013-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reduction_of_graphite_oxide">
       Reduction of graphite oxide
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Reduction of graphite oxide">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Graphite_oxide" title="Graphite oxide">
       Graphite oxide
      </a>
      reduction was probably the first method of graphene synthesis. P. Boehm reported producing monolayer flakes of reduced graphene oxide in 1962.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boehm_179-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Boehm-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Geim acknowledged Boehm's contribution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rapid heating of graphite oxide and exfoliation yields highly dispersed carbon powder with a few percent of graphene flakes. Reduction of graphite oxide monolayer films, e.g. by
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">
       hydrazine
      </a>
      with
      <a href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">
       annealing
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
       argon
      </a>
      /
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
       hydrogen
      </a>
      , was reported to yield graphene films. However, the quality is lower compared to scotch-tape graphene, due to incomplete removal of
      <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">
       functional groups
      </a>
      . Furthermore, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">
       oxidation
      </a>
      protocol introduces permanent defects due to over-oxidation. The oxidation protocol was enhanced to yield
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphene_oxide" title="Graphene oxide">
       graphene oxide
      </a>
      with an almost intact carbon framework that allows efficient removal of functional groups. The measured
      <a href="/wiki/Charge_carrier" title="Charge carrier">
       charge carrier
      </a>
      mobility exceeded 1,000 centimetres (393.70in)/Vs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eigler2013_181-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Eigler2013-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectroscopic" title="Spectroscopic">
       Spectroscopic
      </a>
      analysis of reduced graphene oxide has been conducted.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yamada4_182-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Yamada4-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sugar_method">
       Sugar method
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Sugar method">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Graphene has also been prepared by using a sole sugar(e.g. glucose, fructose, sucrose etc. ), this substrate-free "bottom-up" synthesis method is known as "Tang-Lau method" in which the sole source is sugar, the process is safer, simpler, and more environmentally friendly compared to traditionally top-down method. Another important advantage of Tang-Lau method is control of thickness, ranging from monolayer to multilayers by adjusting growth parameters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_184-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-r1-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Shearing">
       Shearing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Shearing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In 2014 defect-free, unoxidized graphene-containing liquids were made from graphite using mixers that produce local shear rates greater than
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7005100000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        10
       </span>
       <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em">
        
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         4
        </span>
       </sup>
      </span>
      . Commercial graphene products using the output were announced. The method was claimed to be applicable to
      <a href="/wiki/Boron_nitride" title="Boron nitride">
       boron nitride
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum_disulfide" title="Molybdenum disulfide">
       Molybdenum disulfide
      </a>
      and other layered crystals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sonication">
       Sonication
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Sonication">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Applying a layer of graphite oxide film to a
      <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">
       DVD
      </a>
      and burning it in a DVD writer produced a thin graphene film with high electrical conductivity (1738 siemens per meter) and specific surface area (1520 square meters per gram) that was highly resistant and malleable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
       <a href="#cite_note-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        187
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Solvent-aided">
       Solvent-aided
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Solvent-aided">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      Dispersing graphite in a proper liquid medium can produce graphene by
      <a href="/wiki/Sonication" title="Sonication">
       sonication
      </a>
      . Graphene is separated from graphite by
      <a href="/wiki/Centrifugation" title="Centrifugation">
       centrifugation
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
       <a href="#cite_note-188">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        188
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      producing graphene concentrations initially up to
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        6998100000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        0.01
       </span>
       mg/mL
      </span>
      in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-methylpyrrolidone" title="N-methylpyrrolidone">
       N-methylpyrrolidone
      </a>
      (NMP) and later to
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7000210000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        2.1
       </span>
       mg/mL
      </span>
      in NMP,.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
       <a href="#cite_note-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Using a suitable
      <a href="/wiki/Ionic_liquid" title="Ionic liquid">
       ionic liquid
      </a>
      as the dispersing liquid medium produced concentrations of
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7000533000000000000
       </span>
       <span style="white-space:nowrap">
        5.33
       </span>
       mg/mL
      </span>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
       <a href="#cite_note-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene concentration produced by this method is very low, because nothing prevents the sheets from restacking due to van der Waals forces. The maximum concentrations achieved are the points at which the van der Waals forces overcome the interactive forces between the graphene sheets and the solvent molecules.
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Solvent.2Fsurfactant-aided">
       Solvent/surfactant-aided
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Solvent/surfactant-aided">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      Adding a
      <a href="/wiki/Surfactant" title="Surfactant">
       surfactant
      </a>
      to a solvent prior to sonication prevents restacking by adsorbing to the graphene's surface. This produces a higher graphene concentration, but removing the surfactant requires chemical treatments.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Immiscible_liquids">
       Immiscible liquids
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Immiscible liquids">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      Sonicating graphite at the interface of two immiscible liquids, most notably
      <a href="/wiki/Heptane" title="Heptane">
       heptane
      </a>
      and water, producing macro-scale graphene films. The graphene sheets are adsorbed to the high energy interface between the heptane and the water, where they are kept from restacking. The graphene remains at the interface even when exposed to force in excess of 300,000 g. The solvents may then be evaporated. The sheets are up to ~95% transparent and conductive.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
       <a href="#cite_note-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Epitaxy">
       Epitaxy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Epitaxy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Epitaxy" title="Epitaxy">
       Epitaxy
      </a>
      refers to the deposition of a crystalline overlayer on a crystalline substrate, where there is registry between the two. In some cases epitaxial graphene layers are coupled to surfaces weakly enough (by
      <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">
       Van der Waals forces
      </a>
      ) to retain the two dimensional
      <a href="/wiki/Electronic_band_structure" title="Electronic band structure">
       electronic band structure
      </a>
      of isolated graphene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gall1_192-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gall1-192">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        192
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gall2_193-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Gall2-193">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        193
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      An example of weakly coupled epitaxial graphene is the one grown on SiC.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nov_04_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Nov_04-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene monolayers grown on silicon carbide and
      <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">
       iridium
      </a>
      are weakly coupled to these substrates (how weakly remains debated) and the graphenesubstrate interaction can be further passivated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SiCplusH2_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-SiCplusH2-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Silicon_carbide">
       Silicon carbide
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Silicon carbide">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunable_nanoporous_carbon" title="Tunable nanoporous carbon">
       Carbide-derived Carbon
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Heating
      <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">
       silicon carbide
      </a>
      (SiC) to high temperatures (&gt;
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
        7003137315000000000
       </span>
       1100C
      </span>
      ) under low pressures (~10
      <sup>
       6
      </sup>
      torr) reduces it to graphene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
       <a href="#cite_note-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This process produces epitaxial graphene with dimensions dependent upon the size of the wafer. The face of the SiC used for graphene formation, silicon- or carbon-terminated, highly influences the thickness, mobility and carrier density of the resulting graphene.
     </p>
     <p>
      Graphene's electronic band-structure (so-called Dirac cone structure) was first visualized in this material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohta1_195-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ohta1-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohta2_196-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ohta2-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lanzara06_197-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lanzara06-197">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Weak anti-localization is observed in this material, but not in exfoliated graphene produced by the drawing method.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exf_198-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-exf-198">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        198
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Large, temperature-independent mobilities approach those in exfoliated graphene placed on silicon oxide, but lower than mobilities in suspended graphene produced by the drawing method. Even without transfer, graphene on SiC exhibits massless Dirac fermions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ByungHeeHong_85-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ByungHeeHong-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-0908.1900_86-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-0908.1900-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShenAPL_87-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ShenAPL-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-0909.2903_88-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-0909.2903-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-0909.1193_89-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-0909.1193-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phase1_90-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-phase1-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-0909.1220_199-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-0909.1220-199">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        199
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The weak van der Waals force that provides the cohesion of multilayer graphene stacks does not always affect the electronic properties of the individual layers. That is, while the electronic properties of certain multilayered epitaxial graphenes are identical to that of a single layer,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hass1_200-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hass1-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      in other cases the properties are affected,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohta1_195-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ohta1-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohta2_196-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ohta2-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as they are in bulk graphite. This effect is well understood theoretically and is related to the symmetry of the interlayer interactions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hass1_200-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hass1-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Epitaxial graphene on SiC can be patterned using standard microelectronics methods. A band gap can be created and tuned by laser irradiation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
       <a href="#cite_note-201">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        201
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_substrates">
       Metal substrates
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Metal substrates">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The atomic structure of a metal substrate can seed the growth of graphene.
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ruthenium">
       Ruthenium
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Ruthenium">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      Graphene grown on
      <a href="/wiki/Ruthenium" title="Ruthenium">
       ruthenium
      </a>
      does not typically produce uniform layer thickness. Bonding between the bottom graphene layer and the substrate may affect layer properties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhysOrg.com_202-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PhysOrg.com-202">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        202
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Iridium">
       Iridium
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Iridium">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      Graphene grown on
      <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">
       iridium
      </a>
      is very weakly bonded, uniform in thickness and can be highly ordered. As on many other substrates, graphene on iridium is slightly rippled. Due to the long-range order of these ripples, minigaps in the electronic band-structure (Dirac cone) become visible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grIr111_203-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-grIr111-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        203
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nickel">
       Nickel
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Nickel">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      High-quality sheets of few-layer graphene exceeding 1cm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      (0.2sqin) in area have been synthesized via
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition" title="Chemical vapor deposition">
       chemical vapor deposition
      </a>
      on thin
      <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">
       nickel
      </a>
      films using multiple techniques.
     </p>
     <p>
      The growth of graphene on nickel films through
      <a href="/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition" title="Chemical vapor deposition">
       chemical vapor deposition
      </a>
      occurs in a few steps. First the thin nickel film is exposed to
      <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">
       Argon
      </a>
      gas at 9001000 degrees Celsius.
      <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
       Methane
      </a>
      is then mixed into the gas, and the carbon from the methane is absorbed into the nickel film. The nickelcarbon solution is then cooled down in argon gas. During the cooling process the carbon diffuses out of the nickel to form graphene films.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ByungHeeHong_85-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-ByungHeeHong-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
       <a href="#cite_note-204">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        204
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hongR_205-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-hongR-205">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        205
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206">
       <a href="#cite_note-206">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        206
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another used temperatures compatible with conventional
      <a href="/wiki/CMOS" title="CMOS">
       CMOS
      </a>
      processing, using a nickel-based alloy with gold as catalyst.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cmosgraphene_207-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cmosgraphene-207">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        207
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This process dissolves carbon atoms inside a
      <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">
       transition metal
      </a>
      melt at a certain temperature and then precipitates the dissolved carbon at lower temperatures as single layer graphene (SLG).
     </p>
     <p>
      The metal is first melted in contact with a carbon source, possibly a graphite crucible inside which the melt is carried out or graphite powder/chunks that are placed in the melt. Keeping the melt in contact with the carbon at a specific temperature dissolves the carbon atoms, saturating the melt based on the metalcarbon
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_phase_diagram" title="Binary phase diagram">
       binary phase diagram
      </a>
      . Lowering the temperature decreases carbon's solubility and the excess carbon precipitates atop the melt. The floating layer can be either skimmed or frozen for later removal. Using different morphology, including thick graphite, few layer graphene (FLG) and SLG were observed on metal substrate.
      <a href="/wiki/Raman_spectroscopy" title="Raman spectroscopy">
       Raman spectroscopy
      </a>
      proved that SLG had grown on nickel substrate. The SLG Raman spectrum featured no D and D band, indicating its pristine nature. Since nickel is not Raman active, direct Raman spectroscopy of graphene layers on top of the nickel is achievable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shaahin_208-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-shaahin-208">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        208
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another approach covered a sheet of silicon dioxide glass (the substrate) on one side with a nickel film. Graphene deposited via chemical vapor deposition formed into layers on both sides of the film, one on the exposed top side, and one on the underside, sandwiched between nickel and glass. Peeling the nickel and the top layer of graphene left intervening layer of graphene behind on the glass. While the top graphene layer could be harvested from the foil as in earlier methods, the bottom layer was already in place on the glass. The quality and purity of the attached layer was not assessed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209">
       <a href="#cite_note-209">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        209
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h5>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Copper">
       Copper
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Copper">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h5>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">
       Copper
      </a>
      foil, at room temperature and very low pressure and in the presence of small amounts of
      <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
       methane
      </a>
      produces high quality graphene. The growth automatically stops after a single layer forms. Arbitrarily large films can be created.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hongR_205-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-hongR-205">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        205
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CopperGraphene_210-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CopperGraphene-210">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        210
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The single layer growth is also due to the low concentration of carbon in methane. The process is surface-based rather than relying on absorption into the metal and then diffusion of carbon into graphene layers on the surface.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
       <a href="#cite_note-211">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        211
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The room temperature process eliminates the need for postproduction steps and reduces production from a ten hour/nine to ten step procedure to a single step that takes five minutes. A chemical reaction between the hydrogen plasma formed from the methane and ordinary air molecules in the chamber generates
      <a href="/wiki/Cyano_radical" title="Cyano radical">
       cyano radicals
      </a>
      carbonnitrogen molecules without electrons. These charged molecules scour the copper of surface imperfections, providing a pristine substrate. The graphene deposits form lines that merge into each other, forming a seamless sheet that contributes to mechanical and electrical integrity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_212-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-:0-212">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        212
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Larger
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">
       hydrocarbons
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">
       ethane
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">
       propane
      </a>
      produce bilayer coatings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213">
       <a href="#cite_note-213">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        213
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Atmospheric pressure CVD growth produces multilayer graphene on copper (similar to nickel).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lenski_214-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lenski-214">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The material has fewer defects, which in higher temperature processes result from thermal expansion/contraction.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_212-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-:0-212">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        212
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ballistic transport has been observed in the resulting material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
       <a href="#cite_note-215">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        215
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sodium_ethoxide_pyrolysis">
       Sodium ethoxide pyrolysis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Sodium ethoxide pyrolysis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Gram-quantities of graphene were produced by the reduction of
      <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">
       ethanol
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">
       sodium
      </a>
      metal, followed by
      <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">
       pyrolysis
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ethoxide&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ethoxide (page does not exist)">
       ethoxide
      </a>
      product and washing with water to remove sodium salts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
       <a href="#cite_note-216">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        216
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Silicon.2Fgermanium.2Fhydrogen">
       Silicon/germanium/hydrogen
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Silicon/germanium/hydrogen">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      A normal
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicon_wafer" title="Silicon wafer">
       silicon wafer
      </a>
      coated with a layer of
      <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">
       germanium
      </a>
      (Ge) dipped in dilute
      <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluoric_acid" title="Hydrofluoric acid">
       hydrofluoric acid
      </a>
      strips the naturally forming
      <a href="/wiki/Germanium_oxide" title="Germanium oxide">
       germanium oxide
      </a>
      groups, creating hydrogen-terminated germanium. Chemical vapor deposition deposits a layer of graphene on top. The graphene can be peeled from the wafer using a dry process and is then ready for use. The wafer can be reused. The graphene is wrinkle-free, high quality and low in defects.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
       <a href="#cite_note-217">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        217
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
       <a href="#cite_note-218">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        218
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Roll-to-roll">
       Roll-to-roll
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Roll-to-roll">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In 2014 a two-step roll-to-roll manufacturing process was announced that allows scalable graphene production. The first roll-to-roll step produces the graphene via chemical vapor deposition. The second step binds the graphene to a substrate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
       <a href="#cite_note-219">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        219
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220">
       <a href="#cite_note-220">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        220
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Nanotube_slicing">
       Nanotube slicing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Nanotube slicing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Graphene can be created by cutting open
      <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">
       carbon nanotubes
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221">
       <a href="#cite_note-221">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        221
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In one such method
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MWNT" title="MWNT">
       multi-walled carbon nanotubes
      </a>
      are cut open in solution by action of
      <a href="/wiki/Potassium_permanganate" title="Potassium permanganate">
       potassium permanganate
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">
       sulfuric acid
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222">
       <a href="#cite_note-222">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        222
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In another method graphene nanoribbons were produced by
      <a href="/wiki/Plasma_etching" title="Plasma etching">
       plasma etching
      </a>
      of nanotubes partly embedded in a
      <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">
       polymer
      </a>
      film.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223">
       <a href="#cite_note-223">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        223
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide_reduction">
       Carbon dioxide reduction
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide reduction">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A highly exothermic reaction combusts
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
       magnesium
      </a>
      in an oxidationreduction reaction with carbon dioxide, producing a variety of carbon nanoparticles including graphene and
      <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">
       fullerenes
      </a>
      . The carbon dioxide reactant may be either solid (dry-ice) or gaseous. The products of this reaction are carbon and
      <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">
       magnesium oxide
      </a>
      .
      <span class="citation patent">
       <a class="external text" href="http://worldwide.espacenet.com/textdoc?DB=EPODOC&amp;IDX=US8377408" rel="nofollow">
        US patent 8377408
       </a>
      </span>
      <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Apatent&amp;rft.number=8377408&amp;rft.cc=US&amp;rft.title=">
       <span style="display: none;">
       </span>
      </span>
      was issued for this process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224">
       <a href="#cite_note-224">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        224
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spin_coating">
       Spin coating
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Spin coating">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2014, carbon nanotube-reinforced graphene was made via spin coating and annealing functionalized carbon nanotubes. The resulting material was stronger, flexible and more conductive than conventional graphene.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kurz5011_174-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-kurz5011-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Supersonic_spray">
       Supersonic spray
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Supersonic spray">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Supersonic acceleration of droplets through a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laval_nozzle" title="Laval nozzle">
       Laval nozzle
      </a>
      was used to deposit small droplets of reduced graphene-oxide in suspension on a substrate. The droplets disperse evenly, evaporate rapidly and display reduced flake aggregations. In addition, the topological defects (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone-Wales_defect" title="Stone-Wales defect">
       Stone-Wales defect
      </a>
      and
      <span class="chemf" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       C
       <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left">
        <br/>
        2
       </span>
      </span>
      vacancies) originally in the flakes disappeared. The result was a higher quality graphene layer. The energy of the impact stretches the graphene and rearranges its carbon atoms into flawless hexagonal graphene with no need for post-treatment.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225">
       <a href="#cite_note-225">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        225
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226">
       <a href="#cite_note-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        226
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The high amount of energy also allows the graphene droplets to heal any defects in the graphene layer that may occur during this process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227">
       <a href="#cite_note-227">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        227
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Intercalation">
       Intercalation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: Intercalation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Producing graphene via Intercalation splits graphite into single layer graphene by inserting guest molecules/ions between the graphene layers. Graphite was first intercaled in 1841 using a strong oxidizing or reducing agent that damaged the material's desirable properties. Kovtyukhova developed a widely used oxidative intercalation method in 1999. In 2014, she was able to achieve intercalation using non-oxidizing
      <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="BrnstedLowry acidbase theory">
       Brnsted acids
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">
       phosphoric
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfuric" title="Sulfuric">
       sulfuric
      </a>
      , dichloroacetic and alkylsulfonic acids), but without oxidizing agents. The new method has yet to achieve output sufficient for commercialization.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228">
       <a href="#cite_note-228">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        228
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229">
       <a href="#cite_note-229">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        229
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Laser">
       Laser
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Laser">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2014 a laser-based single-step, scalable approach to graphene production was announced. The technique produced and patterned porous three-dimensional graphene film networks from commercial polymer films. The system used a CO2
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_laser" title="Infrared laser">
       infrared laser
      </a>
      . The sp
      <sup>
       3
      </sup>
      -carbon atoms were photothermally converted to sp
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      -carbon atoms by pulsed laser irradiation. The result exhibits high electrical conductivity. The material can produce interdigitated electrodes for in-plane microsupercapacitors with specific capacitances of &gt;4mFcm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      and power densities of ~9mWcm
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      . Laser-induced production appeared to allow roll-to-roll manufacturing processes and provides a route to electronic and energy storage devices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230">
       <a href="#cite_note-230">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        230
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Microwave_Assisted_Oxidation">
       Microwave Assisted Oxidation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: Microwave Assisted Oxidation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2012, a Microwave-assisted, scalable approach was reported to directly synthesize graphene with different size from graphite in one step.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231">
       <a href="#cite_note-231">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        231
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232">
       <a href="#cite_note-232">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        232
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233">
       <a href="#cite_note-233">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        233
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The resulting graphene does not need any post reduction treatment as it contains very low oxygen functionality. This approach avoids use of potassium permanganate in the reaction mixture. It has been also reported that by microwave radiation assistance, graphene oxide with or without holes can be synthesized by controlling microwave time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234">
       <a href="#cite_note-234">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        234
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This method uses a recipe similar to Hummer's method but uses microwave heating instead of traditional heating. Microwave heating can dramatically shorten the reaction time from days to seconds.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">
       Applications
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Applications">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Potential_applications_of_graphene" title="Potential applications of graphene">
       Potential applications of graphene
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      As of 2015, graphene is not used in commercial applications. However, many uses for graphene have been proposed or are under development, in areas including electronics,
      <a href="/wiki/Biological_engineering" title="Biological engineering">
       biological engineering
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Filtration" title="Filtration">
       filtration
      </a>
      , lightweight/strong
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_materials" title="Composite materials">
       composite materials
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">
       photovoltaics
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">
       energy storage
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235">
       <a href="#cite_note-235">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        235
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Graphene is often produced as a powder and as a dispersion in a polymer matrix. This dispersion is supposedly suitable for advanced composites,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236">
       <a href="#cite_note-236">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        236
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      paints and coatings, lubricants, oils and functional fluids, capacitors and batteries, thermal management applications, display materials and packaging, inks and 3D-printers materials, and barriers and films.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237">
       <a href="#cite_note-237">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        237
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Health_risks">
       Health risks
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Health risks">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Research at Brown university found that 10m few-layered graphene flakes are able to pierce cell membranes in solution. They were observed to initially enter via sharp and jagged points, allowing graphene to be internalized in the cell. The physiological effects of this remain uncertain, and this remains a relatively unexplored field.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238">
       <a href="#cite_note-238">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        238
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239">
       <a href="#cite_note-239">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        239
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
       References
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Graphene&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: References">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGraphene&amp;rft.atitle=Surface+Properties+of+Extremely+Thin+Graphite+Lamellae&amp;rft.au=Boehm%2C+H.+P.&amp;rft.au=Clauss%2C+A.&amp;rft.aufirst=H.+P.&amp;rft.au=Fischer%2C+G.&amp;rft.au=Hofmann%2C+U.&amp;rft.aulast=Boehm&amp;rft.date=1962&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fgraphenetimes.com%2Fwp-content%2Fuploads%2F1961%2F09%2FBoehmProcCarbon1962.pdf&amp;rft.pub=Pergamon+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         <span class="error citation-comment" style="font-size:100%">
          Missing or empty
          <code style="color:inherit; border:inherit; padding:inherit;">
           |title=
          </code>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#citation_missing_title" title="Help:CS1 errors">
           help
          </a>
          )
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-32">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-32">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         This paper reports graphitic flakes that give an additional contrast equivalent of down to ~0.4nm or 3 atomic layers of amorphous carbon. This was the best possible resolution for 1960 TEMs. However, neither then nor today it is possible to argue how many layers were in those flakes. Now we know that the TEM contrast of graphene most strongly depends on focusing conditions.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meyer07_30-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Meyer07-30">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           30
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
         For example, it is impossible to distinguish between suspended monolayer and multilayer graphene by their TEM contrasts, and the only known way is to analyse relative intensities of various diffraction spots. The first reliable TEM observations of monolayers are probably given in refs. 24 and 26 of
         <a href="#CITEREFGeimNovoselov2007">
          Geim &amp; Novoselov 2007
         </a>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-Oshima97-33">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-Oshima97_33-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation journal">
          Oshima, C.; Nagashima, A. (1997). "Ultra-thin epitaxial films of graphite and hexagonal boron nitride on solid surfaces".
          <i>
           J. Phys.: Condens. Matter
          </i>
          <b>
           9
          </b>
          : 120.
          <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">
           Bibcode
          </a>
          :
          <a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1997JPCM....9....1O" rel="nofollow">
           1997JPCM....9....1O
          </a>
          .
          <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
           doi
          </a>
          :
          <a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1088%2F0953-8984%2F9%2F1%2F004" rel="nofollow">
           10.1088/0953-8984/9/1/004
          </a>
          .
         </span>

       <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGraphene&amp;rft.atitle=The+rise+of+graphene&amp;rft.aufirst=A.+K.&amp;rft.au=Geim%2C+A.+K.&amp;rft.aulast=Geim&amp;rft.au=Novoselov%2C+K.+S.&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2007NatMa...6..183G&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2Fnmat1849&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F17330084&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature+Materials&amp;rft.pages=183-91&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=6">
        <span style="display:none;">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
     </ul>
